Nikah — Marriage
beginner

Umm Walad: Son's Slave Girl Bears Master's Child
Ruling on umm walad from son's slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a son's slave girl gives birth to the master's child, and identify the obligations of the father.

Core Idea
A son's slave girl becomes umm walad for the father if she bears his child, requiring the father to pay her value.

This lesson covers the first of three rulings from the text. The scenario involves a son's slave girl who bears a child from the son's father (the master). The Hanafi school establishes specific consequences: she becomes an umm walad, the father must compensate the son by paying her value, and no mahr is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and ownership
The slave girl is owned by the son. The father is the master in relation to the son, but not the owner of the slave girl. The child is born from the father.
Ownership distinction is key: the father does not own the slave girl outright.
2
Determine the consequence of childbirth
Because the child is from the father (the master of the son, not the slave girl's owner), she becomes an umm walad. This status means she will be freed upon the father's death.
Umm walad status requires that the child be from the master of the mother. Here, the father is considered the master in a certain sense.
3
Identify financial obligations
The father must pay the value of the slave girl to the son as compensation, because the father effectively took away the son's property by making her umm walad. No mahr is due because a slave cannot own mahr.
The payment of value is wajib; mahr is not applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تو مہبر مولی کیلئے ہے
بیٹے کی باندی سے ولی کی ، اس نے بچہ جنا تو یہ ام ولد ہے اور باپ پر اس کی قیمت لازم ہے اور مہر اس پر نہیں۔ بیٹے نے اپنی باندی کا نکاح باپے کر دیا، اس نے بچہ جنا تو ام ولد نہ ہوگی اور نہ باپ پر قیمت لازم ہوگی مہر لازم ہوگا اور اولاد آزاد ہوگی
آزاد عورت کسی غلام کی منکوحہ ہو پھر عورت نے مولی شوہر سے کہا کہ اس کو میری طرف سے ایک ہزار کے بدلے آزاد کر دے تو مولی نے ایسا کر دیا تو نکاح فاسد ہے عورت نے کہا آزاد کر دو لیکن مال مقر ر نہیں کیا تو نکاح فاسد نہیں ہوگا اور ولاء معتق کیلئے ہوگا
The master's mahr is for the master? If a son's slave girl gives birth to the master's child, she becomes umm walad and the father must pay her value, but no mahr is due. If the son marries his slave girl to his father and she gives birth, she does not become umm walad, the father owes no value but must pay mahr, and the child is free. A free woman married to a slave then says to her husband's master: 'Free him for a thousand from me,' and the master does so, then the marriage is invalid (fasid). If she says 'free him' without specifying any wealth, the marriage is not invalid, and the wala' belongs to the manumitter.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the table of contents summarizing key Hanafi rulings on two distinct scenarios. First scenario: If a son's slave girl gives birth to a child from the son's father (the master), she becomes an umm walad (mother of the master's child), entitling her to freedom upon the master's death. The father must pay her value as compensation to the son, but no mahr is due because a slave cannot own property. Second scenario: If the son marries his slave girl to his father and she bears a child, she does not become an umm walad because valid marriage (nikah) takes precedence over mere ownership of a share in her. Thus the father must pay the mahr, and the child is free through the father. Third scenario: A free woman married to a slave asks the slave's master to free him for a price; if she specifies a payment (here one thousand), the marriage becomes fasid (invalid) because the woman effectively bought her husband, which is prohibited. If she does not specify payment, the marriage remains valid and the wala' (patronage) goes to the manumitter (the master). These rules highlight Hanafi distinctions between milk al-yamin and nikah.

Key Terms
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child from her master; she becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.
نکاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage that is defective due to a temporary impediment; it can be rectified or must be terminated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario, what must the father pay?
A) Mahr
B) The value of the slave girl
C) Nothing
D) Both mahr and value

Key Takeaway
Conception by the master makes a son's slave girl umm walad, requiring the father to pay her value to the son.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage of Son's Slave Girl to Father: No Umm Walad
Ruling on son's slave girl married to father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a son's slave girl married to his father does not become umm walad, and state the consequences (mahr, child's status).

Core Idea
Marriage (nikah) overrides milk al-yamin, so a son's slave girl married to his father becomes a free woman in terms of marriage, not umm walad, requiring mahr and freeing the child.

This lesson addresses the second scenario: the son marries his slave girl to his father. Unlike the first case where the father directly impregnates his son's slave, here the slave girl is married to the father. The Hanafi ruling changes dramatically: she does not become umm walad, the father must pay her mahr, and the child is free. Understanding the distinction between conception through ownership and through marriage is key.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the relationship
The son marries his slave girl to his father. The father is the husband, not the owner. The marriage is valid because a slave can marry, but the father now has both marriage and a property interest? No, the father does not own her; the son does.
The father becomes a husband, not a master of the slave girl.
2
Determine why she does not become umm walad
Umm walad status arises only if the child is born from the master's own slave, not from a wife. Since the father is married to her, she is his wife, not his slave. Hence no umm walad status.
The prohibition of collecting ownership and marriage? Actually, the father can marry a slave, but the child from such marriage is free and the mother is not umm walad.
3
Identify financial obligations and status of child
The father must pay mahr (dower) because marriage requires mahr. The child is free because he follows the father (free). No payment of value is due because she does not become umm walad.
Mahr is wajib; the child's freedom is automatic.

Source Text

Free Woman's Payment to Free Slave Husband: Effect on Marriage
Ruling on free woman buying her slave husband's freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a marriage when a free woman secures her slave husband's freedom by paying his master, and identify when the marriage becomes invalid.

Core Idea
A free woman's marriage to a slave becomes invalid (fasid) if she pays the master a specified amount to free him, but remains valid if no payment is specified.

This lesson covers the third scenario: a free woman married to a slave asks her husband's master to free him, offering a specific amount (e.g., one thousand). If she names a price, the marriage becomes fasid (invalid) because she effectively buys her husband. If she simply asks for his freedom without specifying payment, the marriage remains valid and the wala' (patronage) goes to the master who frees him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key condition
The woman says to the master: 'Free him for a thousand from me.' She specifies a sum. This constitutes a purchase of the husband by the wife.
The condition is that the woman provides the consideration for the husband's freedom.
2
Determine the effect on marriage
Because she pays the master to free her husband, the marriage becomes fasid (irregular). The reason: a wife cannot become the owner of her husband; buying him would create a conflict of ownership and marriage.
Fasid means defective but not void ab initio; it must be separated.
3
Contrast with unspecified request
If she simply says 'Free him' without offering money, the marriage remains valid. The master may free him out of generosity, and the wala' goes to the master (the manumitter).
No purchase means no conflict; the free woman remains married and the wala' is earned by the master.

Source Text

Validity of Prior Marriage After Conversion and Incestuous Unions
Opening section on validity of polytheist marriages after conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for continuity of a prior polytheist marriage upon conversion and apply it to incestuous unions.

Core Idea
A marriage valid in polytheist law continues after conversion unless it violates Islamic prohibitions like incest.

When polytheists embrace Islam, their prior marriages are generally upheld if they were valid in their own religion. However, incestuous marriages – such as a Magian marrying his mother or daughter – are automatically annulled. The Hanafi school distinguishes between defects in form (like missing witnesses) and substantive prohibitions. Let's explore the key scenarios from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the opening question: if a disbeliever marries without witnesses or during iddah (permitted in their religion), and both convert, is the marriage valid? The text presents statements of jurists; note the principle that the prior contract is respected unless it contravenes a clear Islamic prohibition.
The answer depends on whether the defect is purely procedural or substantive.
2
Focus on incestuous marriages
Locate the specific case: a Magian marries his mother or daughter, then both convert. According to the passage, they are separated (furqah). This is because incest is absolutely prohibited in Islam, overriding any prior validity.
This is an example of a substantive prohibition that voids the marriage regardless of prior law.
3
Understand the role of the judge
When only one spouse converts, the passage states: the judge presents Islam to the non-Muslim spouse. If they accept, the marriage continues; otherwise, separation is ordered. This procedural step ensures the non-Muslim spouse has the opportunity to embrace Islam before dissolution.
This applies when the non-Muslim spouse is a dhimmi or musta'min, not a harbi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب نکاح اهل الشرك
کسی کا فرنے بغیر گواہوں یا کسی کافر کی عدت میں نکاح کیا اور یہ ان کے دین میں جائز ہے پھر دونوں مسلمان ہو گئے پہلا نکاح برقرار ہے یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء
صورت مسئلہ
مجوسی نے اپنی ماں سے یا بیٹی سے نکاح کیا پھر دونوں مسلمان ہو گئے تو دونوں میں تفریق کی جائے گی دونوں میں سے ایک کے اسلام سے تفریق کی جائے گی اور مرافعہ کی صورت میں تفریق نہیں کی جائے گی مرتد کا مسلمان کافرہ ، مرتدہ عورت سے نکاح جائز نہیں
زوجین میں سے ایک مسلمان ہو تو بچہ مسلمان ہو گا
عورت مسلمان ہوگئی اور اس کا خاوند کا فر ہے قاضی اس پر اسلام پیش کرے گا اگر مسلمان ہو گیا تو عورت بیوی برقرار رہے گی ور نہ دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کی جائے گی ، اقوال فقہاء عورت دار الحرب میں مسلمان ہوگئی اور شوہر کا فر ہے یا حربی مسلمان ہو گیا اور اس کی بیوی مجوسیہ ہے فرقت ہوگی یا نہیں فرقت واقع ہوگئی اور عورت حربیہ ہے اس پر عدت نہیں ، مسلمان عورت کا بھی یہی حکم ہے، اقوال فقہاء زوجین میں سے ایک دارالحرب سے مسلمان ہو کر دار الاسلام آ گیا دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کی جائے گی ، امام شافعی کا نقط نظر ۱۸۵ عورت ہجرت کر کے دارالاسلام آگئی اس سے بغیر عدت کے نکاح جائز ہے یا نہیں، اقوال فقہاء
زوجین میں سے کوئی ایک مرتد ہو گیا تو بغیر طلاق کے فرقت واقع ہوگی ، اقوال فقہاء زوجین دونوں اکٹھے مرتد ہوئے پھر ا کٹھے مسلمان ہوئے نکاح برقرار رہے گا ، امام زفر کا نقطہ نظر
Chapter on marriage of polytheists. If a disbeliever marries without witnesses or in the 'iddah of a disbeliever and it is valid in their religion, then both become Muslim, is the first marriage valid? Statements of jurists. Case scenario: A Magian marries his mother or daughter, then both become Muslim, they are separated. If one of them becomes Muslim, separation occurs. In case of mutual conversion, no separation. Marriage of a Muslim with an apostate woman is not permissible. If one spouse becomes Muslim, the child is Muslim. A woman becomes Muslim and her husband is a disbeliever; the judge presents Islam to him. If he becomes Muslim, the marriage remains; otherwise separation. Statements of jurists. A woman becomes Muslim in dar al-harb while husband remains disbeliever, or a harbi becomes Muslim while his wife is Magian: does separation occur? Separation occurs, and if the woman is harbiyyah, no 'iddah is required. The same ruling applies to a Muslim woman. Statements of jurists. If one spouse becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and comes to dar al-Islam, they are separated. Imam al-Shafi'i's view. A woman migrates to dar al-Islam; is it permissible to marry her without 'iddah? Statements of jurists. If one spouse apostatizes, separation occurs without divorce. Statements of jurists. If both apostatize together and then both become Muslim together, the marriage remains. Imam Zufar's view.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This is a ruling statement covering the chapter on marriage of polytheists (ahl al-shirk). The passage summarizes multiple scenarios: (1) A marriage valid in the disbelievers' religion but lacking Islamic conditions (witnesses or 'iddah) – if both convert, the validity is disputed among Hanafi jurists. (2) Incestuous marriages (mother-daughter) are annulled upon conversion. (3) If one spouse converts, the judge must invite the other; acceptance keeps marriage, refusal causes separation. (4) In dar al-harb, conversion leads to immediate separation, and if the non-Muslim spouse is harbiyyah (enemy), no 'iddah is required. (5) Apostasy: if one apostatizes, separation occurs instantly without divorce; if both apostatize and return together, marriage remains according to Imam Zufar. The passage also notes the view of Imam al-Shafi'i regarding migration and 'iddah. These rulings highlight Hanafi principles regarding continuity of prior contracts after conversion and the effect of apostasy on marriage.

Key Terms
فرقت (furqah): Separation between spouses, which may occur automatically or by court order.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after dissolution of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a Magian marries his daughter and then both become Muslim, what happens?
A) The marriage is valid because it was valid in their religion.
B) The marriage is annulled (separation).
C) They must renew the marriage with Islamic witnesses.
D) The marriage continues but they are required to separate after consummation.

Key Takeaway
A prior polytheist marriage continues after conversion unless it involves a relationship that is permanently prohibited in Islam, such as incest.

Conversion in Dar al-Harb, Iddah, and Apostasy
Rulings on conversion in dar al-harb and apostasy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for separation upon conversion in dar al-harb, determine iddah obligations, and analyze the effect of apostasy on marriage.

Core Idea
Conversion in dar al-harb immediately separates spouses, with no iddah for a harbiyya; apostasy of one spouse also separates instantly without divorce.

When conversion occurs in enemy territory (dar al-harb), the Hanafi school rules that separation takes place immediately, unlike in dar al-Islam where the judge invites the non-Muslim spouse. Furthermore, if the non-Muslim spouse is a harbiyya (enemy woman), no iddah is required. Apostasy directly dissolves the marriage without need for divorce, but joint apostasy and return preserves the marriage. Let's examine these important exceptions and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish dar al-Islam from dar al-harb
In dar al-Islam, when one spouse converts, the judge invites the other to Islam. In dar al-harb, there is no judge, so separation occurs automatically upon conversion. Read the line: 'عورت دار الحرب میں مسلمان ہوگئی... فرقت واقع ہوگئی'.
The key is the absence of Islamic authority in dar al-harb.
2
Note the iddah exception for harbiyya
If the non-Muslim wife is a harbiyya (enemy combatant), the passage states 'اس پر عدت نہیں' (no iddah upon her). This is because she is not subject to Islamic law and the separation is considered a dissolution by force majeure.
This differs from the usual iddah requirement for Muslim women or dhimmis.
3
Apostasy and its effect on marriage
The passage clearly states: if one spouse apostatizes, separation occurs without divorce (بغیر طلاق کے فرقت واقع ہوگی). Apostasy severs the marital bond because a Muslim cannot remain married to an apostate.
However, if both apostatize together and then both return to Islam together, the marriage is preserved (Imam Zufar's view, also accepted by most Hanafis).

Source Text

کسی کا فرنے بغیر گواہوں یا کسی کافر کی عدت میں نکاح کیا اور یہ ان کے دین میں جائز ہے پھر دونوں مسلمان ہو گئے پہلا نکاح برقرار ہے یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء
صورت مسئلہ
مجوسی نے اپنی ماں سے یا بیٹی سے نکاح کیا پھر دونوں مسلمان ہو گئے تو دونوں میں تفریق کی جائے گی دونوں میں سے ایک کے اسلام سے تفریق کی جائے گی اور مرافعہ کی صورت میں تفریق نہیں کی جائے گی مرتد کا مسلمان کافرہ ، مرتدہ عورت سے نکاح جائز نہیں
زوجین میں سے ایک مسلمان ہو تو بچہ مسلمان ہو گا
عورت مسلمان ہوگئی اور اس کا خاوند کا فر ہے قاضی اس پر اسلام پیش کرے گا اگر مسلمان ہو گیا تو عورت بیوی برقرار رہے گی ور نہ دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کی جائے گی ، اقوال فقہاء عورت دار الحرب میں مسلمان ہوگئی اور شوہر کا فر ہے یا حربی مسلمان ہو گیا اور اس کی بیوی مجوسیہ ہے فرقت ہوگی یا نہیں فرقت واقع ہوگئی اور عورت حربیہ ہے اس پر عدت نہیں ، مسلمان عورت کا بھی یہی حکم ہے، اقوال فقہاء زوجین میں سے ایک دارالحرب سے مسلمان ہو کر دار الاسلام آ گیا دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کی جائے گی ، امام شافعی کا نقط نظر ۱۸۵ عورت ہجرت کر کے دارالاسلام آگئی اس سے بغیر عدت کے نکاح جائز ہے یا نہیں، اقوال فقہاء
زوجین میں سے کوئی ایک مرتد ہو گیا تو بغیر طلاق کے فرقت واقع ہوگی ، اقوال فقہاء زوجین دونوں اکٹھے مرتد ہوئے پھر ا کٹھے مسلمان ہوئے نکاح برقرار رہے گا ، امام زفر کا نقطہ نظر
Chapter on marriage of polytheists. If a disbeliever marries without witnesses or in the 'iddah of a disbeliever and it is valid in their religion, then both become Muslim, is the first marriage valid? Statements of jurists. Case scenario: A Magian marries his mother or daughter, then both become Muslim, they are separated. If one of them becomes Muslim, separation occurs. In case of mutual conversion, no separation. Marriage of a Muslim with an apostate woman is not permissible. If one spouse becomes Muslim, the child is Muslim. A woman becomes Muslim and her husband is a disbeliever; the judge presents Islam to him. If he becomes Muslim, the marriage remains; otherwise separation. Statements of jurists. A woman becomes Muslim in dar al-harb while husband remains disbeliever, or a harbi becomes Muslim while his wife is Magian: does separation occur? Separation occurs, and if the woman is harbiyyah, no 'iddah is required. The same ruling applies to a Muslim woman. Statements of jurists. If one spouse becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and comes to dar al-Islam, they are separated. Imam al-Shafi'i's view. A woman migrates to dar al-Islam; is it permissible to marry her without 'iddah? Statements of jurists. If one spouse apostatizes, separation occurs without divorce. Statements of jurists. If both apostatize together and then both become Muslim together, the marriage remains. Imam Zufar's view.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This is a ruling statement covering the chapter on marriage of polytheists (ahl al-shirk). The passage summarizes multiple scenarios: (1) A marriage valid in the disbelievers' religion but lacking Islamic conditions (witnesses or 'iddah) – if both convert, the validity is disputed among Hanafi jurists. (2) Incestuous marriages (mother-daughter) are annulled upon conversion. (3) If one spouse converts, the judge must invite the other; acceptance keeps marriage, refusal causes separation. (4) In dar al-harb, conversion leads to immediate separation, and if the non-Muslim spouse is harbiyyah (enemy), no 'iddah is required. (5) Apostasy: if one apostatizes, separation occurs instantly without divorce; if both apostatize and return together, marriage remains according to Imam Zufar. The passage also notes the view of Imam al-Shafi'i regarding migration and 'iddah. These rulings highlight Hanafi principles regarding continuity of prior contracts after conversion and the effect of apostasy on marriage.

Key Terms
فرقت (furqah): Separation between spouses, which may occur automatically or by court order.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after dissolution of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a woman in dar al-harb becomes Muslim and her husband remains a harbi, is she required to observe iddah?
A) Yes, she must observe iddah like any Muslim woman.
B) No, because the husband is a harbi and no iddah is required for a harbiyya.
C) It depends on whether she migrates to dar al-Islam.
D) She must observe iddah if they had consummated the marriage.

Key Takeaway
Conversion in dar al-harb and apostasy of one spouse cause immediate separation without iddah for the harbiyya; joint apostasy and return preserves the marriage.

Nikah — Marriage

The Dual Nature of Nikah and Structural Hierarchy in al-Hidayah
Opening of Kitab al-Nikah with virtues and structural definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Nikah combines worship and transaction, and differentiate between Kitaab, Baab, and Fasl in al-Hidayah's organizational scheme.

Core Idea
Nikah unites worship and transaction; Kitaab, Baab, Fasl form a genus-species-individual hierarchy.

This passage opens the Book of Nikah with a profound insight into the nature of marriage. It explains why the author of al-Hidayah positioned Nikah between pure worship and pure transactions: because it embodies both dimensions. The passage also clarifies the book's internal organization using a genus-species-individual analogy, helping readers navigate the fiqh material. Additionally, several ahadith establish the merit and recommended status of marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions of Nikah
From the text, note that Nikah has aspects of worship (preferred over nafl prayers, protects from zina) and transaction (involves mahr, ijab/qabul, testimony, qadi).
The dual nature is why it is placed in the middle of the book.
2
Understand the author's organizing principle
Recognize that al-Hidayah is arranged from pure ibadat to pure muamalat, with mixed topics in between. Nikah is the prime example of a mixed topic.
3
Analyze the ahadith on virtues
Extract the key proofs that marriage is a sunnah mu'akkadah and a means of completing faith. The hadiths establish its merit.
These ahadith are the basis for the recommended ruling.
4
Parse the structural hierarchy
Distinguish Kitaab (genus), Baab (species), Fasl (individual) and how they relate to the fiqh content. Kitaab groups issues of different realities, Baab groups similar realities, Fasl deals with a single issue.
This hierarchy is crucial for navigating the text.
5
Apply this structure to reading
When encountering any Kitaab, Baab, or Fasl, identify its scope and level of specificity to understand the relationship between headings and rulings.
Practice by examining other chapters in al-Hidayah.

(۴ چار چیزیں جس کو ملیں اس کو دنیا اور آخرت کی بھلائی علی منجملہ ان میں صالح عورت ہے جو شو ہر کی خیانت نذکرے، نہ اس کے مال میں نہ اپنے نفس میں ۔ (۵) جس نے نکاح کر لیا اس نے آدھا ایمان بچالیا۔ پھر آدھے کے بچانے میں اللہ تعالیٰ سے تقویٰ رکھے۔ (بہیقی) (۲) جو شخص کسی عورت کے ساتھ اس کی عزت کی وجہ سے نکاح کرتا ہے تو اللہ تعالیٰ کچھ نہیں دیتا سوائے ذلت کے اور یونہی مالداری کے خیال سے ہو تو محتاجی اور حسب کے خیال سے ہو تو کمینگی بڑھتی ہے۔ اور اگر عفت کے خیال سے ہو تو اللہ تعالے برکت دیتا ہے۔ یہ بات بھی پیش نظر رہے کہ صاحب ہدایہ نے عنوانات میں تین لفظ ذکر فرمائے ہیں ۔ کتاب ، باب فصل ، کتاب بمنزلہ جنس کے ہے (جس کے تحت ایسے بہت سے مسائل مذکور ہوتے ہیں جن کی حقیقتیں مختلف ہیں۔ باب بمنزلہ نوع کے ہے (جس کے تحت ایسے بہت سے مسائل مذکور ہوتے ہیں جن کی حقیقت ایک ہو) فصل بمنزلہ شخص کے ہے جس کے تحت کسی معین مشخص مسئلہ کو ذکر کیا جاتا ہے۔
The Book of Nikah. 18. Ashraf al-Bidayah, Urdu commentary of Bidayah, Volume Four. Introduction. The author of Hidayah arranged the book such that first he stated the issues of pure acts of worship (like prayer, fasting, etc.). At the end, he stated the rulings of pure transactions (like sales, pre-emption, etc.). In the middle, he stated issues that contain both the meaning of worship and transaction. For example, Nikah has the meaning of worship because engaging in Nikah is more meritorious than engaging in supererogatory prayers. Also, in Nikah, there is protection of the soul from falling into adultery. Nikah has the meaning of transaction because in it there is wealth that is a substitute for ownership of the vagina; also there is offer and acceptance, testimony, and the need for a judge's decree. Virtues of Nikah: There are two acts of worship that have been legislated from the time of Adam until today and will remain continuously in Paradise: one is faith, the second is Nikah. Hadith: (1) Whoever turns away from my Sunnah (i.e., Nikah) is not from me. (Saheeh) (2) The best of the enjoyment of the world is a righteous woman. (Muslim) (3) Four things are from the Sunnah of the prophets: applying antimony, using perfume, miswak, and Nikah. (Tirmidhi) (Tabarani) (4) Whoever is given four things has been given the good of this world and the Hereafter, including a righteous woman who does not betray her husband in his wealth or in herself. (5) Whoever has married has completed half of his faith. Then let him fear Allah regarding the remaining half. (Bayhaqi) (2) [sic] Whoever marries a woman for her prestige, Allah gives him nothing but disgrace; similarly, if it is for wealth, it leads to poverty; if for lineage, it leads to meanness. And if it is for the sake of chastity, Allah gives blessing. Also, bear in mind that the author of Hidayah has used three words in his headings: Kitaab, Baab, Fasl. Kitaab is like a genus (under which many issues with different realities are mentioned). Baab is like a species (under which many issues with one reality are mentioned). Fasl is like an individual (under which a specific, defined issue is mentioned).
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This is the introductory section (tamheed) to the Book of Nikah in Ashraf al-Hadaya. The passage has two main parts. First, it explains the overall arrangement of al-Hidayah: the author (al-Marghīnānī) placed pure acts of worship (al-‘ibādāt al-maḥḍah) at the beginning, then pure transactions (al-mu‘āmalāt al-maḥḍah) at the end, and in between he placed topics that combine aspects of both worship and transaction. Nikah is the prime example: it is an act of worship because it is preferred over supererogatory prayers and safeguards one from sin; it is also a transaction because involves a dower (mahr) which is a substitute for the marital right, contract formation (ijāb wa qabūl), testimony, and potential need for a qāḍī's ruling. This categorization shows the multi-faceted nature of marriage in Hanafi fiqh. Second, the passage quotes several aḥādīth on the virtues of marriage, establishing its importance and recommending it. These aḥādīth serve as proofs for the sunnah status of Nikah. Finally, the text clarifies the author's internal structure of the book: 'Kitaab' is a broad heading analogous to a genus (jins) encompassing topics of different realities; 'Baab' is a sub-heading like a species (naw‘) covering issues of one reality; 'Fasl' is a more specific section like an individual (shakhṣ) discussing a single, defined issue. This helps the reader understand the organization of the fiqh content. The comment '18' in the original text likely refers to the page number of the original Hidayah or the volume number? In this edition, it is indicated as page 18.

Key Terms
کتاب (Kitaab): A major heading in the book, like a genus (jins), under which many issues with different natures are collected.
باب (Baab): A sub-heading under a kitaab, like a species (naw‘), under which issues with a single common nature are grouped.
فصل (Fasl): A specific section under a baab, like an individual (shakhṣ), dealing with a single, well-defined issue.
نکاح (Nikāh): Marriage; in this context, it is an act that has both worship and transaction aspects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the tamheed, why does the author of Hidayah place the chapter on Nikah in the middle of the book, between pure worship and pure transactions?
A) Because Nikah is the most important topic
B) Because Nikah combines aspects of both worship and transaction
C) Because the author forgot to put it elsewhere
D) Because it is a disliked act

Key Takeaway
Nikah is a unique blend of worship and transaction, and the Kitaab-Baab-Fasl hierarchy helps organize fiqh rulings from general to specific.

Definition and Legal Types of Nikah
Defines nikah linguistically and legally, then categorizes its rulings based on individual circumstances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi classification of nikah (sunnah mu'akkadah, wajib, makruh) and apply it to different scenarios based on ability, desire, and risk of harm.

Core Idea
The ruling of nikah depends on the individual's ability, desire, and risk of sin or neglect.

Nikah is not a one-size-fits-all obligation or recommendation. The Hanafi school teaches that its ruling shifts according to your circumstances. In this lesson, we will first understand the definition of nikah—from its literal meaning to its legal transfer—and then master the three possible rulings: sunnah mu'akkadah, wajib, and makruh.

Guided Path
1
Break down the linguistic derivation
Trace the word nikah from its literal meaning 'joining' to its legal meaning 'marriage contract' through two transfers (to intercourse, then to contract).
This helps you see why the contract is central.
2
Identify the three rulings
List the three shari’i rulings: sunnah mu'akkadah, wajib, and makruh.
Memorise these categories.
3
Match each ruling to its condition
For each ruling, identify the precise condition that triggers it: ability (for sunnah), intense desire/fear of zina (for wajib), predominant assumption of oppression/neglect (for makruh).
The conditions are the key to application.
4
Consider cross-madhhab differences
Note that other schools (e.g., Shafi’i) do not classify nikah as wajib; for them it is always a recommended act.
This explains why fatwa may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح
ترجمہ یہ کتاب احکام نکاح کے بیان میں
تشریح نکاح لغت میں قسم کے لئے ہے۔ پھر وہلی کے معنی میں نقل کر لیا گیا۔ ولی کے ضم پر مشتمل ہونے کی وجہ سے ۔ پھر عقد نکاح کی
طرف نقل کر لیا گیا۔ اس لئے کہ عقد سبب ضم ہے۔ گویا عقد مجاز المجاز ہے۔
نکاح کی اقسام نکاح شرعی کی تین قسمیں ہیں۔ سنت مؤکدہ واجب ، مکروہ۔ مہر، نفقہ اور وطی پر قدرت کی صورت میں نکاح سنت مؤکدہ ہے۔ عورتوں کی طرف شدت اشتیاق کے وقت واجب ہے۔ جس وقت ظلم کا غالب گمان ہو اور فرائض وسنن کے ترک کا تو ایسی صورت میں
نکاح مکروہ ہے۔
The Book of Nikah. Translation: This book is about the rulings of nikah. Explanation: Nikah literally means 'joining together' (ضم). Then it was transferred to the meaning of 'intercourse' (وطء) because it involves conjunction. Then it was transferred to the marriage contract because the contract is a cause for conjunction. So the contract is a metaphor of a metaphor. Types of Nikah: Shar'i nikah has three types: Sunnah Mu'akkadah, Wajib, and Makruh. If one has the ability for mahr, nafaqah, and intercourse, nikah is Sunnah Mu'akkadah. When there is intense desire for women, it is Wajib. When there is a predominant assumption of oppression and neglect of obligatory and sunnah acts, in that situation nikah is Makruh.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling classification. It first explains the etymology of nikah: literal meaning 'joining', then transferred to intercourse, then to the contract (metaphor of metaphor). Then it categorizes the legal ruling of nikah into three types based on the individual's situation: Sunnah Mu'akkadah (highly recommended) when one can fulfill financial and physical responsibilities; Wajib (obligatory) when one fears falling into sin due to intense desire; and Makruh (disliked) when one is likely to neglect religious duties or oppress. This classification shows that the ruling is not fixed but depends on the person's circumstances.

Key Terms
نکاح (nikāḥ): Legally, the marriage contract. Literally: joining, transferred to intercourse, then to contract.
سنت مؤکدہ (sunnah muʾakkadah): A recommended act that is strongly emphasized; neglecting it without reason is blameworthy.
واجب (wājib): Obligatory; required by Shari'ah; leaving it is sinful.
مکروہ (makrūh): Disliked; an act that should be avoided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When one has the ability to provide mahr and nafaqah.
B) When there is intense desire for women (fear of zina).
C) When one fears neglecting prayers.
D) When one has a strong lineage.

Key Takeaway
The ruling of nikah in the Hanafi school is not fixed; it depends on the individual's ability, desire, and risk of sin or neglect.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner

Ruling: Offer and Acceptance Must Be in Past Tense
Explains why the marriage contract requires words in the past tense for validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement that offer and acceptance in a marriage contract must be spoken in the past tense, and explain the reasoning based on the legal transfer (naql) of past tense from khabar to insha'.

Core Idea
The marriage contract must be spoken in past tense: by legal necessity, past-tense words create the present legal effect.

In Hanafi law, the validity of a marriage contract depends on the precise wording of offer and acceptance. This lesson unpacks the requirement that both must be uttered in the past tense, and the ingenious legal reasoning—based on linguistic transfer—that justifies it. We'll see why writing alone is insufficient and how analogy to supplication phrases resolves a potential contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
What does the matn state about the form of offer and acceptance? Note the phrase 'with two words that express the past tense'.
The ruling is that spoken past-tense words are required; writing alone does not suffice.
2
Recognize the objection
Why might someone object that the past tense is inappropriate for creating a contract? Because the past tense normally reports a completed action, while a contract creates a present effect.
The objection arises from the literal meaning of the past tense (madi).
3
Understand the solution: legal transfer (naql)
How does the Hanafi school resolve the objection? It argues that the past tense has been legally transferred from its original usage (khabar) to insha' (creation) in certain contexts, by necessity.
This transfer is not arbitrary; it is based on common usage in supplications and praise/blame verbs.
4
Follow the qiyas argument
What is the analogy? Just as in supplications like 'saqa Allahu tharah' the past tense is used for insha', so too in marriage contracts, the past tense is used for insha' out of need.
The need (haja) is for an unambiguous, immediate creation of the contract.
5
Conclude the ruling
What is the practical outcome? The marriage contract is valid only if spoken in past tense; writing is insufficient. This is unanimously held by the three Hanafi imams.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text

عقد نکاح کیلئے ایجاب وقبول کی شرعی حیثیت
قال النكاح ينعقد بالايجاب والقبول بلفظين يعبر بهما عن الماضى لان الصيغة وان كانت للاخبار وضعا فقد جعلت للانشاء شرعادفعا للحاجة
ترجمہ نکاح متحقق ہونا ہے ایسے دو لفظوں کی وجہ سے جن کو صیغہ ماضی سے تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے اس لیے کہ صیغہ ماضی اگر چہ اخبار کیلئے موضوع ہے لیکن شرعا انشاء کیلئے نقل کر لیا گیا ضرورت کو دور کرنے کے لئے۔
تشریح ایجاب اس لفظ کو کہتے ہیں جو احد المتعاقدین سے اولاً صادر ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ وہ مخاطب پر اثبات یا نفی میں جواب کو واجب کرتا ہے۔ اور جو لفظ احد المتعاقدین سے ثانیا صادر ہوگاوہ قبول کہلائے گاماتن نے بلفظین فرما کر اس طرف اشارہ فرمادیا کہ صرف تحریری ایجاب و قبول سے نکاح منعقد نہیں ہوگا۔ یہی قول ائمہ ثلثہ کا بھی ہے کیونکہ تحریری ایجاب وقبول کو نقوش تو کہا جاسکتا ہے لفظ نہیں لان الصيغة . سے دلیل کی صورت میں اشکال کا جواب دیا گیا ہے۔ حاصل اشکال یہ ہے کہ نکاح انشاء کے قبیل سے ہے اور انشاء کہتے ہیں اثبات ما لم یکن ثابتا کو یعنی هیچیز ثابت نہیں تھی اس کو ثابت کرنا اور حدوث امر فی الحال کو یعنی زمانہ موجودہ میں کسی امر کو عدم سے وجود میں لانا لہذا انعقاد نکاح ایسے لفظ کا محتاج ہو گا جو صراحنا اس معنی پر دلالت کرے اور لغت میں ایسا لفظ موجود نہیں جو صراحتاً حدوث امر فی الحال پر دلالت کرے کیونکہ ماضی تو گزشتہ پر دلالت کرتی ہے اور مضارع جس طرح حال پر دلالت کرتا ہے استقبال پر بھی دلالت کرتا ہے۔ لہذا مضارع کی دلالت حال پر صریحی نہیں ہوگی تو اب ایجاب و قبول کو صیغہ ماضی سے کیسے تعبیر کیا جاسکتا ہے۔ جواب دیا گیا کہ صیغہ ماضی گواخبار کے لئے وضع کیا گیا ہے لیکن مصالحہ نکاح کی ضرورت کو پورا کرنے کے لئے انشاء کے معنی میں نقل کیا گیا۔ جیسے جملہ دعائیہ سقى الله ثراہ اور افعال مدح نعم اور زم بنس میں ماضی کو انشاء کے معنی میں نقل کر لیا گیا۔ واللہ اعلم
Legal status of offer and acceptance for the marriage contract. He (the author) said: Nikah is contracted by offer and acceptance using two words that express the past tense. Because although the form (sighah) is originally for reporting (khabar), it has been legally transferred for creation (insha') to fulfill the need. Translation: Nikah occurs by two words that are expressed in the past tense, because although the past tense form is placed for reporting, it has been transferred legally for creation to address the need. Explanation: Ijab is the word that first emanates from one of the two contracting parties, because it makes the response obligatory on the addressee in affirmative or negative. The word that emanates second from the other party is called qabul. The matn said 'with two words' indicating that a written offer and acceptance alone will not contract nikah. This is also the view of the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), because written offer and acceptance can be called marks (nuqush) but not words. Then an objection is answered using 'lanna al-sighah'. The gist of the objection: Nikah is of the type of insha' (creation), and insha' means establishing what was not established, i.e., bringing something into existence from non-existence in the present moment. Therefore, the contract of nikah requires a word that clearly indicates this meaning, but in language there is no word that explicitly indicates occurrence in the present because the past tense indicates past, and the present tense indicates both present and future, so its indication of the present is not explicit. So how can offer and acceptance be expressed in the past tense? The answer: Although the past tense is placed for reporting, it has been transferred to the meaning of insha' to fulfill the need of the marriage contract, just as in supplicatory sentences like 'saqa Allahu tharah' (may Allah water his dust) and in verbs of praise (ni'ma) and blame (bi'sa), the past tense is used for insha'. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that the marriage contract must be concluded with words (spoken, not written) in the past tense (e.g., 'I married you' and 'I accept'). The matn (from al-Hidayah) says 'with two words that express the past tense'. The commentator explains that 'bisfayn' (with two words) indicates that writing alone is insufficient—a view agreed upon by the three Hanafi imams. Then an objection is raised: nikah is an insha' (creation of a legal effect in the present), but the past tense normally reports the past, and the present tense is ambiguous (could be present or future). So how can past tense work for insha'? The answer: By necessity, the past tense has been legally transferred (naql shar'i) to mean insha' in the context of contracts, similar to its use in supplications and praise/blame verbs. This is an example of istihsan based on necessity (haja). The Hanafi school maintains that spoken words are essential; mere writing does not constitute a valid offer and acceptance.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ījāb): The offer; the first statement by one contracting party that necessitates a response.
قبول (qabūl): The acceptance; the second statement by the other contracting party that completes the contract.
صیغہ ماضی (sīghah māḍī): Past tense verbal form, used customarily for reporting but transferred for creating contracts (insha').
انشاء (inshāʾ): Creation; bringing a legal effect into existence at the moment of utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is easier to pronounce.
B) Because the present tense is ambiguous, and the past tense is transferred to insha' by necessity.
C) Because the Quran uses past tense for marriage.
D) Because the Prophet always used past tense.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires spoken past-tense words for a valid marriage contract, based on the legal transfer of the past tense from reporting to creation by necessity.

Contracting Marriage with Past and Future Tense
Ruling on validity of marriage with non-past tenses and one-person contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marriage contracted with past and imperative tenses and explain the rational basis (qiyas) distinguishing marriage from sale regarding one person acting as both parties.

Core Idea
Marriage is valid with past and imperative tenses; one person can handle both ijab and qabul because rights revert to the principal.

This passage from al-Hidayah explains a unique feature of the marriage contract in Hanafi law: it can be concluded using a past tense word (e.g., 'zawwijtuk') and an imperative word (e.g., 'zawwijni'), where the imperative is treated as a delegation. More remarkably, a single person—acting as principal for themselves and agent for the other—can pronounce both the offer and acceptance. This flexibility is justified through qiyas (analogy) that distinguishes marriage from sale based on where contractual rights ultimately return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that marriage is contracted with two words: one in the past tense and the other expressing the future (here meaning the imperative). Recognize that the Hanafi school permits this combination.
The text explicitly uses 'yusta'qbal' to mean the imperative form.
2
Analyze the example
Consider: A man says 'zawwijni' (marry me) and the woman responds 'zawwijtuk' (I married you). Identify that 'zawwijni' is a delegation (tawkeel) to act as agent, not an ijab itself. The second statement serves as both ijab (offer) and qabul (acceptance) because the speaker is acting for herself as principal and for the man as agent.
The agent's response simultaneously completes the contract.
3
Examine the qiyas distinction
Compare marriage to sale. In sale, one person cannot handle both sides because rights in sale return to the agent, leading to a logical contradiction (same person being demander and demanded, transferor and transferee). In marriage, rights return to the principal (the one who delegated), so no such contradiction occurs.
The illa (effective cause) is the direction of reversion of rights.
4
Understand the rational basis
Reflect on why the difference matters: In sale, if one person acts for both, the agent would be both the one demanding payment and the one being demanded, causing impossibility. In marriage, the agent's actions are for the benefit of the principal, so the agent can logically represent both sides without conflict.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on preventing logical absurdity.

Source Text

And it is contracted with two words, one expressing the past and the other the future, such as when he says 'zawwijni' and he says 'zawwijtuk', because this (zawwijni) is a delegation in marriage, and one person undertakes both sides of the marriage as we will explain, God willing. Translation: And marriage is contracted with two words such that one is expressed in the past tense and the other in the future tense. For example, someone says 'zawwijni', the other responds 'zawwijtuk'. This is because 'zawwijni' is a delegation for marriage, and one person can undertake both sides of marriage (ijab and qabul), God willing. We will explain this later. Commentary: In the text, 'future' refers to the imperative form. The result is that marriage is contracted by the past tense and the imperative. For example, a man says 'marry me', the woman responds 'I married you' or vice versa, then the marriage is contracted. This is because the first statement is not ijab but rather appointing an agent for marriage, and the second statement (zawwajtu) is both ijab and qabul. In marriage, one of the two contracting parties can perform both ijab and qabul because he is the principal regarding himself and the agent regarding the other. In the chapter of marriage, one person can undertake both sides of ijab and qabul, although this is not correct in the chapter of sale. The reason for the difference is that in sale, rights return to the agent, while in marriage, they return to the principal. So in sale, if one of the two contracting parties undertakes both sides, then the same person would be both the demander and the demanded, the transferor and the transferee, which is irrational and impossible. But because in marriage rights return to the principal, this defect does not arise. Details will come later.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Hidayah states a ruling: marriage is contracted with two words, one in the past tense and the other in the future (imperative). The commentator explains that the first word (e.g., 'zawwijni') is a delegation (tawkeel) to perform the marriage, and the second word ('zawwijtuk') serves as both ijab and qabul together. The key point is that in marriage, one person can handle both sides—as principal for himself and agent for the other—unlike in sale. The reason is that in sale, rights return to the agent, causing a conflict where the same person would be both the one demanding and the one being demanded, and both the transferor and the transferee, which is logically impossible. In marriage, since rights return to the principal (the one who delegated), no such conflict arises. The distinction is based on the different nature of the two contracts. The commentator notes that the text's 'future' means the imperative form, so the ruling includes the imperative as well as the past. This section lays the foundation for understanding the flexibility in the form of the marriage contract.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ijab): The offer or declaration from one party in a contract.
قبول (qabul): The acceptance from the other party in a contract.
توکیل (tawkeel): Delegation or appointment of an agent to perform an act.
ماضی (madi): Past tense verb, e.g., 'zawwijtuk' (I married you).
مستقبل (mustaqbal): Future tense, but here it refers to the imperative form (amr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage contracts are valid using past and imperative tenses, and one person can perform both ijab and qabul through delegation because rights return to the principal, not the agent.

Nikah — Marriage

Validity of Marriage with Sale and Lease Words
Ruling with proof on marriage with non-standard words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marriage with the words bay', ijarah, ibahah, ihlaal, i'aarah, and wasiyyah, and explain the proof based on causal connection (majaz) for bay' and its absence for ijarah.

Core Idea
Marriage is valid with 'sale' due to a causal link to usufruct, but not with 'lease' which lacks this link.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hadaya addresses whether marriage is contracted using words from other contracts, such as sale (bay'), lease (ijarah), permission (ibahah), making lawful (ihlaal), lending (i'aarah), and bequest (wasiyyah). The correct Hanafi position distinguishes between words that cause ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'ah) and those that do not. We will unpack the reasoning behind these rulings and the internal Hanafi disagreements on bay' and ijarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine which contract words are being evaluated for their ability to contract marriage. The passage examines six specific words: bay', ijarah, ibahah, ihlaal, i'aarah, and wasiyyah.
2
State the ruling for each
According to the correct view (saheeh qawl): marriage is valid only with the word 'bay''. The other five words do not contract marriage. Note the internal Hanafi disagreements: Abu Bakr al-A'mash rejected bay', and Imam al-Karkhi accepted ijarah.
3
Analyze the proof for bay'
The proof relies on the concept of majaz (figurative usage) through causality. Bay' (sale) transfers ownership of the raqabah (corporeal object), which in turn causes ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'ah) – the same effect as nikah. Thus, using the cause (bay') for the effect (milk al-mut'ah) is a valid figurative extension.
Example: Selling a slave-girl transfers her raqabah to the buyer, who then owns her usufruct, including sexual enjoyment (without impediments).
4
Explain why ijarah is rejected
Ijarah (lease) transfers only the usufruct (manfa'ah) for a specified period, but this usufruct does not include the specific usufruct of tamattu' (sexual enjoyment) found in marriage. Therefore, there is no causal link from ijarah to milk al-mut'ah. Hiring a slave-girl for service does not permit intercourse. Hence, no majaz is possible.
Imam al-Karkhi argued that ijarah also involves usufruct, but the response is that the usufructs differ in kind.
5
Note the remaining words
Ibahah (permission), ihlaal (making lawful), i'aarah (lending), and wasiyyah (bequest) similarly do not cause milk al-mut'ah. Wasiyyah is a transfer deferred to after death, so it cannot contract an immediate marriage.

وينعقد بلفظة الجيع هو الصحيح لوجود طريق المجاز ولا ينعقد بلفظة الاجارة فى الصحيح لانه ليس بسبب لملك المتعة ولا بلفظة الاباحة والاحلال والاعارة لماقلنا ولا بلفظة الوصية لانها توجب الملک مضافا الى ما بعد الموت

And it is contracted with the word bay' - this is the correct view due to the presence of the way of majaz. And it is not contracted with the word ijarah in the correct view, because it is not a cause for ownership of usufruct. Nor with the word ibahah, nor ihlaal, nor i'aarah for what we said. Nor with the word wasiyyah, because it necessitates ownership attributed to after death. Translation: And marriage is contracted with the word bay' - this is the correct view due to the existence of the way of majaz. And it is not contracted with the word ijarah in the correct view, because ijarah is not a cause for ownership of usufruct. Nor with the word ibahah, nor ihlaal, nor i'aarah for the reason stated. Nor with the word wasiyyah, because the word wasiyyah necessitates ownership that is attributed to after death. Commentary: According to the view of Abu Bakr al-Asamm, marriage is contracted with the word bay'. Abu Bakr al-A'mash says it is not contracted. The reason is that the word bay' is specific to the transfer of property for property, and the object transferred in nikah is not property, so marriage will not be contracted. The correct view is that here also the way of majaz exists, in that bay' necessitates a ownership that is a cause for ownership of usufruct. Because when someone sells a slave-girl, the buyer becomes the owner of her 'raqabah' and also within that the owner of her usufruct, and intercourse with her becomes permissible besides service, provided there is no prohibition. From the word ijarah, in the correct view, marriage is not contracted. Imam al-Karkhi says it is contracted. Imam al-Karkhi gave the reason that ijarah is transfer of usufruct (manfa'ah), and ownership of usufruct is also a usufruct. But we will respond: ownership of usufruct (tamattu') is not among the usufructs of ijarah; if a slave-girl is hired for service, it is not permissible for the lessee to have intercourse with her. The proof of the correct view is that ijarah is not a cause for ownership of usufruct; therefore, due to the absence of the connection of causality, the way of majaz is not found. With the words ibahah, ihlaal, i'aarah, the same is the reason that these words are not causes for ownership of usufruct. With the word wasiyyah also... (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of marriage using words other than the standard ones. The correct view (saheeh qawl) is that marriage is contracted with the word 'bay'' (sale) due to the existence of a figurative connection (majaz) through causality: selling a slave-girl transfers ownership of her person (raqabah), which in turn causes ownership of her usufruct (tamattu'), the same effect as marriage. Therefore, using the word 'bay'' to mean marriage is valid. However, Abu Bakr al-A'mash (or al-Asamm? The text mentions both; it says 'قول اصبح' which may be 'Qawl al-Asamm' and then 'ابو بکر اعمش' which is Abu Bakr al-A'mash) disagreed, arguing that the object of sale is property, while the object of marriage is not property. The correct view overrides this. For 'ijarah' (lease), the correct view is that marriage is NOT contracted because ijarah is not a cause for ownership of usufruct (tamattu'). Imam al-Karkhi argued that both ijarah and marriage involve usufruct, but the response is that the usufruct in marriage (sexual enjoyment) is not included in the usufructs of ijarah—if you hire a slave-girl for service, intercourse is not permissible. Therefore, no causal link exists. Similarly, words like ibahah (permission), ihlaal (making lawful), i'aarah (lending), and wasiyyah (bequest) are not causes for ownership of usufruct, so marriage is not valid with them. The passage is incomplete but the rulings are clear.

Key Terms
بیع (bay'): Sale contract, considered valid for marriage via figurative usage due to causal link.
اجارہ (ijarah): Lease or hire contract; not valid for marriage because it does not cause ownership of usufruct (tamattu').
ملک متعہ (milk al-mut'ah): Ownership of usufruct, specifically sexual enjoyment in marriage.
منفعت (manfa'ah): Usufruct; in ijarah, it includes service but not the specific usufruct of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is marriage not contracted with the word 'ijarah' according to the correct Hanafi view?
A) Because ijarah is a temporary contract, while marriage is permanent.
B) Because ijarah does not cause ownership of usufruct (tamattu') - for example, hiring a slave-girl for service does not make intercourse lawful.
C) Because the word ijarah is not Arabic.
D) Because Imam al-Karkhi said it is valid but the majority disagree.

Key Takeaway
The validity of marriage with non-standard words depends on whether the word implies a causal link to immediate ownership of the specific usufruct of marriage.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage with Non-Standard Terms: Ruling on Bay', Ijara, etc.
Hanafi rulings on validity of marriage using sale, lease, and other terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage contracted with words like bay', ijara, ibaha, etc. and explain the underlying principle of sababiyyah (causal link to milk al-mut'ah).

Core Idea
Marriage requires a word that causes immediate ownership of intimacy; sale works, lease does not.

The marriage contract is typically concluded with the explicit words 'nikah' or 'tazwij'. However, what if someone uses the word 'sale' (bay') or 'lease' (ijara)? In the Hanafi school, the validity depends on whether the term establishes a causal link (sababiyyah) to the ownership of marital intimacy (milk al-mut'ah). This lesson unpacks the rulings for each term, along with the intra-madhhab disagreements and the logical reasoning behind the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key question and the correct position
The passage asks which terms (bay', ijara, ibaha, ihlal, i'arah, wasiyyah) can validly contract a marriage. The correct position (sahih) is that bay' works; the others do not.
The text labels the ruling 'sahih' for bay' and 'la yan'aqid' for others.
2
Understand the analogy (qiyas) for bay'
The reasoning: sale (tamlik mal bil-mal) of a slave-girl gives ownership of her person (raqabah), which includes ownership of her usufruct (milk al-mut'ah). Therefore, the word 'sale' metaphorically indicates the same causal link to marital intimacy.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on sababiyyah.
3
Examine the intra-madhhab objection to bay'
Abu Bakr al-A'mash argued that sale is specific to property, and marriage is not property, so no validity. The majority responded that the existence of the metaphor (majaz) is sufficient because the causal link exists in the sale of a slave-girl.
The Hanafi response invokes the principle of 'tariq al-majaz' (path of metaphor) based on commonality of cause.
4
Analyze why ijara does not work
Imam al-Karkhi analogized lease to sale: lease transfers usufruct, and milk al-mut'ah is a usufruct. But the Hanafi response distinguishes: lease transfers a specific usufruct (e.g., service), not the comprehensive ownership of the person's usufruct. Thus, no causal link.
The key is that ijarah does not grant ownership of the person (raqabah); it only grants a limited right to a defined benefit.
5
Apply the same reasoning to ibaha, ihlal, i'arah, and wasiyyah
These terms also lack the causal link to immediate ownership of marital intimacy. 'Permission' and 'making lawful' do not transfer ownership; 'loan' does not transfer ownership of the person; 'will' transfers ownership only after death, but marriage requires immediate ownership.
Consistency: all invalid terms fail the sababiyyah test.

Source Text

Does marriage take place with the words sale (bay'), lease (ijara), permission (ibaha), making lawful (ihlal), will (wasiyyah) and loan (i'arah)?
‘And it takes place with the word ‘sale’ – this is the correct position – due to the existence of the path of metaphor. And it does not take place with the word ‘lease’ in the correct position because it is not a cause for ownership of usufruct. And not with the word ‘permission’, ‘making lawful’, and ‘loan’ due to what we said. And not with the word ‘will’ because it obligates ownership added to after death.’
Translation: Marriage is contracted with the word ‘sale’ – this is the correct saying – due to the existence of the path of metaphor. And it is not contracted with the word ‘lease’ in the correct saying because lease is not a cause for ownership of usufruct. And not with the word ‘permission’, nor ‘making lawful’, nor ‘loan’ for the reason stated. And not with the word ‘will’ because the word will obligates ownership that is attributed to after death. Explanation: Based on the correct saying, marriage is contracted with the word ‘sale’. Abu Bakr al-A'mash says that it will not be contracted with it. The reason is that the word ‘sale’ is specific to transferring property for property, and that which is owned through marriage is not property, so marriage would not take place. The reason for the correct saying is that here too the path of metaphor exists, in that sale is a means to a property that is a cause for ownership of usufruct. Because when someone sells a slave-girl, the buyer, within the property of her person, also becomes owner of her usufruct, and sexual intercourse becomes permissible for him besides service, provided there is no cause of prohibition. With the word ‘lease’, based on the correct saying, marriage will not be contracted. Imam al-Karkhi says that it will be contracted. Imam al-Karkhi stated the reason that lease is the transfer of usufruct, and ownership of usufruct is also a usufruct. But we will reply: ownership of usufruct is not among the usufructs of lease. Thus, if a slave-girl is taken on lease for service, sexual intercourse is not permissible for the lessee. The proof of the correct saying is that lease is not a cause for ownership of usufruct, hence due to the absence of causal relationship, the path of metaphor was not found. With the words ‘permission’, ‘making lawful’, and ‘loan’, the reason for marriage not taking place is the same: these words are also not causes for ownership of usufruct. With the word ‘will’ also, marriage will not be contracted because although will causes ownership, it causes ownership that is obtained after death, and for contracting marriage, ownership that exists at the time is needed.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on which words can be used for the marriage contract beyond the standard nikah and tazwij. It first establishes the correct position: marriage is valid with the word ‘sale’ (bay‘). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Bakr al-A‘mash argues that sale is specific to property exchange and marriage is not property, but the majority holds that the same causal relationship (sababiyyah) applies: sale of a slave-girl gives ownership of her person and thereby ownership of her usufruct, making permissible intimacy. Thus, using ‘sale’ is a valid metaphor. For ‘lease’ (ijarah), the correct position is non-validity, against Imam al-Karkhi who analogizes it to transfer of usufruct. The response is that ijarah transfers use of a specific usufruct (like service), not the comprehensive usufruct of marriage. So causality is lacking. Similarly, ‘permission’, ‘making lawful’, and ‘loan’ do not establish ownership of usufruct and thus are invalid. ‘Will’ (wasiyyah) grants ownership only after death, but marriage requires immediate ownership at contract time, so it is invalid. The underlying principle is consistent: valid words must have a causal link (sababiyyah) to milk al-mut‘ah (ownership of marital intimacy) at the time of contract.

Key Terms
تملیک مال بالمال (tamlik mal bil-mal): Transfer of property in exchange for property (sale).
ملک متعہ (milk al-mut‘ah): Ownership of usufruct, specifically the right to sexual intimacy in marriage.
طریق مجاز (tariq al-majaz): Path of metaphor; the linguistic mechanism that allows a word to be used in a non-literal sense when a causal link exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because lease transfers only a specific usufruct (like service), not the comprehensive usufruct of marriage (milk al-mut‘ah).
C) Because lease requires consideration, but marriage is a gift.
D) Because Imam al-Karkhi ruled it invalid.

Key Takeaway
A marriage contract is valid only if the word used directly causes immediate ownership of marital intimacy; sale works because it includes the person's usufruct, but lease, permission, and will do not.

Conditions for Witnesses in Marriage Contract
Hanafi conditions for witnesses: freedom, sanity, puberty, Islam, and gender

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not require justice ('adālah) or masculinity for marriage witnesses and identify the conditions it does require.

Core Idea
Witnesses are mandatory for marriage; but Hanafis require only free, sane, adult, Muslim witnesses—justice and masculinity are not required.

The Hanafi school holds a distinctive position on witnesses in marriage, emphasizing that justice ('adālah) is not a condition and that one man and two women suffice. This lesson unpacks the required conditions—freedom, sanity, adulthood, and Islam—based on the hadith 'No marriage except with witnesses' and the principle of wilāyah (legal capacity). We also explore the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi‘i, who argues that testimony is a form of honor requiring moral probity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact conditions listed for marriage witnesses: free, sane, adult, Muslim; two men or one man and two women; justice not required.
The passage begins with a clear statement from al-Quduri.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Imam al-Shafi‘i differs on two points: he requires justice in witnesses and may also require two male witnesses or specific gender conditions.
The text says 'وفيه خلاف الشافعى' regarding one man and two women.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi‘i argues that testimony is a matter of honor (karāmah) and a fāsiq (immoral person) is dishonorable, hence unfit.
The opposing dalil is based on moral character.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that a fāsiq has wilāyah over himself due to his Islam, so he can exercise it over others; he can also give a mandate (taqlīd), so he qualifies as a witness.
The analogy of the blind and sons of contractors shows that bearing (taḥammul) is valid even if performance (adā') is restricted.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis validate marriage with two fāsiq witnesses or one man and two women; Shafi‘is require just witnesses and likely two men or one man and two just women.
This affects the validity of marriages contracted in communities where such witnesses are used.

گواہوں کی شرعی حیثیت
قال ولا ينعقد نكاح المسلمين الابحضور شاهدين عاقلين حرین بالغین مسلمین رجلين اورجل وامرأتين عدولا كانوا او غیر عدول او محدودين فى القذف قال اعلم ان الشهادة شرط في باب النكاح لقوله عليه السلام لانكاح الا بشهود وهو حجة على مالك في اشتراط الاعلان دون الشهادة ولابد من اعتبار الحرية فيها لان العبد لا شهادة له لعدم الولاية ولابد من اعتبار العقل والبلوغ لانه لاولاية بدونهما ولابد من اعتبار الاسلام فى انكحة المسلمين لانه لا شهادة للكافر على المسلم ولا يشترط وصف الذكورة حتى ينعقد بحضور رجل وامرأتين وفيه خلاف الشافعى وستعرف فى الشهادات ان شاء الله ولا تشترط العدالة حتى ينعقد بحضرة الفاسقين عندنا خلافا للشافعى له ان الشهادة من باب الكرامة والفاسق من اهل الاهانة ولنا انه من اهل الولاية فيكون من اهل الشهادة وهذا لانه لمالم يحرم الولاية على نفسه لاسلامه لا يحرم على غيره لانه من جنسه و لانه صلح مقلد افيصلح مقلد او كذا شاهد او المحدود فى القذف من اهل الولاية فيكون من اهل الشهادة تحملا وانما الفائت ثمرة الاداء بالنهى لحريمته ولايبالى بفواته كما فى شهادة العميان وابني العاقدين
ترجمہ مسلمانوں کا نکاح نہیں منعقد ہوتا ہے مگر ایسے دو گواہوں کی موجودگی میں جو آزاد ہوں ، عاقل ہوں، بالغ ہوں، مسلمان ہوں ، دونوں مرد ہوں یا ایک مرد اور دو عورتیں عادل ہوں یا غیر عادل، یا محدود فی القذف ہوں ۔ مصنف نے فرمایا کہ توجہ سے سنو شھادت باب نکاح میں شرط ہے حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کے فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ نکاح نہیں مگر گواہوں کے ساتھ اور یہ قول نبی حجت ہے امام مالک کے خلاف اعلان کی شرط لگانے میں نہ کہ شہادت کی اور ضروری ہے آزادی کا اعتبار شہادت میں ۔ کیونکہ غلام کے لئے شہادت نہیں ولایت کے نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اور عقل و بلوغ کا ہونا بھی ضروری ہے۔ کیونکہ بغیر ان دونوں کے ولایت نہیں۔ اور ضروری ہے مسلمان ہونے کا اعتبار مسلمانوں کے نکاح میں۔ اس لئے کہ مسلمان کے خلاف کا فر کو شہادت کا حق نہیں ہے ۔ اور نہیں شرط لگائی گئی مذکر ہونے کی ۔ چنانچہ ایک مرد اور دو عورتوں کی موجودگی میں نکاح منعقد ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس بارے میں امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے۔ عنقریب کتاب الشہارات میں آپ کو معلوم ہو جائے گا انشاءاللہ اور عدالت کی بھی شرط نہیں لگائی گئی۔ چنانچہ دوفاستوں کی موجودگی میں نکاح منعقد جائے گا ہمارے نزدیک خلاف ہے امام شافعی کا۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہادت باب کرامت سے ہے اور فاسق اہل اہانت سے ہے۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ فاسق اہل ولایت سے ہے لہذا اہل شہادت سے بھی ہو گا ۔ اور یہ بات اس وجہ سے ہے کہ جب فاسق اپنی ذات پر ولی ہونے سے محروم نہیں کیا گیا مسلمان ہونے کی وجہ سے تو غیر پر ولی ہونے سے بھی محروم نہیں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ یہ غیر بھی اس کا ہم جنس ہے۔ اور اس لئے کہ فاسق صلاحیت رکھتا ہے کہ دوسرے کو قلادہ پہنا دے پس خود بھی صاحب قلادہ ہوسکتا ہے اور ایسے ہی شاہد بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور محدود فی القذف اہل ولایت سے ہے لہذا اہل شہادت سے بھی ہوگا از راہ حمل اور فوت ثمرہ ادا ہے مخالفت کی وجہ سے جو اس کے مجرم
Legal status of witnesses
He (al-Quduri) said: The marriage of Muslims is not contracted except in the presence of two witnesses: sane, free, adult, Muslim, two men or one man and two women, whether they are just or not, or even if they are hadd-punished for false accusation (mahrūm). He said: Know that testimony is a condition in the chapter of marriage due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'No marriage except with witnesses.' And this is a proof against Malik regarding his condition of publicity (i‘lān) without testimony. Freedom must be considered in it because a slave has no testimony due to lack of legal capacity (wilāyah). Sanity and adulthood must be considered because there is no wilāyah without them. Islam must be considered in the marriages of Muslims because a non-Muslim has no testimony against a Muslim. The quality of masculinity is not required, so it is contracted in the presence of one man and two women, and in this there is opposition from al-Shafi‘i, and you will know in the Book of Testimonies, God willing. And justice (‘adālah) is not required, so it is contracted in the presence of immoral persons (fāsiqīn) according to us, contrary to al-Shafi‘i. For him, testimony is from the category of honor (karāmah) and the immoral person is from the people of insult (ihānah). For us: he is from the people of legal capacity (wilāyah), so he is from the people of testimony. This is because since he is not prohibited from having wilāyah over himself due to his Islam, he is not prohibited over others because they are of his type. And because he is fit to make a necklace (i.e., to give a mandate), so he is fit to be a necklace-holder (i.e., a witness). Similarly, the one who is hadd-punished for false accusation is from the people of wilāyah, so he is from the people of testimony in terms of bearing (taḥammul), and the only loss is the result of performance (adā’) due to prohibition for his sanctity, and its loss is not cared about, just like the testimony of the blind and the sons of the contractors.
Translation: The marriage of Muslims is not contracted except in the presence of two witnesses who are free, sane, adult, Muslim, both men or one man and two women, whether they are just or unjust, or hadd-punished for false accusation. The author said: Listen carefully; testimony is a condition in the chapter of marriage due to the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'No marriage except with witnesses.' And this saying of the Prophet is a proof against Imam Malik, who stipulates publicity (i‘lān) instead of testimony. Freedom must be considered in testimony because a slave has no testimony due to lack of wilāyah. Sanity and adulthood are also necessary because without these two there is no wilāyah. And it is necessary to consider being Muslim in the marriages of Muslims because a non-Muslim has no right of testimony against a Muslim. And masculinity is not stipulated; thus marriage is contracted in the presence of one man and two women. Concerning this, Imam al-Shafi‘i has a difference. Soon in the Book of Testimonies you will know, God willing. And justice is also not stipulated; thus marriage is contracted in the presence of two immoral persons according to us, contrary to Imam al-Shafi‘i. Imam al-Shafi‘i's proof is that testimony is from the category of honor and the immoral person is from the people of insult. Our proof is that the immoral person is from the people of legal capacity (wilāyah), so he will also be from the people of testimony. This is because when the immoral person is not prevented from being a guardian over himself due to his Islam, then he will not be prevented from being a guardian over others because this other is of his same type. And because the immoral person has the capability to put a necklace on another (i.e., give a mandate), so he himself can be a necklace-holder (i.e., a witness). And similarly, the one hadd-punished for false accusation is from the people of wilāyah, so he will be from the people of testimony in terms of bearing (taḥammul), and the loss is only of the result of performance (adā’) due to the prohibition for his sanctity, and its loss is not cared about.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage details the Hanafi conditions for witnesses in the marriage contract. It begins with the basic requirement: two witnesses who are free, sane, adult, Muslim, either two men or one man and two women. Justice (adālah) is not required, so even a fāsiq (immoral person) or one who has been punished for qadhf (false accusation) can serve. The author refutes Imam Malik who only requires publicity (i‘lān) without witnesses, citing the Hadith: 'No marriage except with witnesses.' Then for each condition: freedom is needed because a slave lacks wilāyah (legal capacity); sanity and adulthood are prerequisites for wilāyah; Islam is required because a non-Muslim cannot testify against a Muslim. Masculinity is not a condition, as one man and two women suffice, contra Shafi‘i who requires two men. The dispute with Shafi‘i over justice is then addressed: Shafi‘i argues that testimony is a matter of honor (karāmah) and a fāsiq is dishonorable. The Hanafi response is that a fāsiq still has wilāyah over himself due to his Islam, and this extends to others of his type. Additionally, a fāsiq can give a mandate (taqlīd), so he can also be a witness. For the one punished for qadhf, his wilāyah is intact for bearing testimony (taḥammul), though performance (adā’) may be compromised; but this is overlooked as it is a practical loss not affecting validity.

Key Terms
ولایت (wilāyah): Legal capacity to act on one's own behalf or on behalf of others; here, the basis for being a witness.
عدالت (adālah): Justice, moral probity; not required for witnesses in marriage according to Hanafis.
محدود فی القذف (mahrūm fī al-qadhf): One who has been hadd-punished for false accusation of adultery; such a person is still eligible to bear witness (tahammul) according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a condition for witnesses in marriage?
A) Freedom (ḥurriyyah)
B) Justice ('adālah)
C) Islam
D) Sanity ('aql)

Key Takeaway
Witnesses in a Hanafi marriage must be free, sane, adult, and Muslim, but need not be just or male; the fāsiq retains legal capacity (wilāyah) to bear testimony.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Conditions of Witnesses for Marriage: Hanafi Position and Disputes
Explains witness requirements for Muslim marriage and disputes with Malik and Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for marriage witnesses, explain why a fasiq qualifies based on wilaya, and distinguish the Hanafi position from those of Imam Malik and Imam Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A fasiq has wilaya over himself, so he qualifies to witness a marriage—uprightness is not required.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah sets out the Hanafi requirements for marriage witnesses, but more importantly it reveals the school's method of legal reasoning: using wilaya (authority) as the criterion for witness qualification. The Hanafis argue against both Imam Malik (who said publicity suffices) and Imam Shafi'i (who demands uprightness). By the end of this lesson, you will see how a single principle—wilaya—generates a coherent set of rules for marriage witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The marriage of Muslims is not valid except in the presence of witnesses meeting specific conditions: free, sane, adult, Muslim, two men or one man and two women.
This is the foundational rule from which all discussions follow.
2
Examine the dispute with Malik
Imam Malik considered publicity (i'lan) sufficient without witnesses. The Hanafis use the hadith 'La nikaha illa bi shuhud' to argue that witnesses are a condition (shart), not just a recommendation.
The Hanafis distinguish between obligation (wujub) and condition (shart) in this context.
3
Analyze the dispute with Shafi'i on uprightness
Shafi'i argues that witnesses must be 'udul (upright) because testimony is an honor (karamah) and a fasiq deserves disgrace (ihanah). The Hanafi response: wilaya is not negated by sin; a fasiq still has authority over himself and others of his kind, so he qualifies as a witness.
This is the key point of the passage: wilaya, not moral character, determines witness qualification in marriage.
4
Apply the wilaya principle to the mahdud fi al-qadhf
A person punished for false accusation of adultery (qadhf) still has wilaya, so he can bear testimony (tahammul) at the marriage contract. However, he cannot perform testimony (ada') in court due to the Quranic prohibition (Surah al-Nur, 24:4).
This shows that the capacity to bear testimony is sufficient for marriage; court testimony is a separate matter.
5
Understand the analogy with blind persons and sons of contracting parties
Similarly, blind persons and sons can witness a marriage (they have tahammul) but their testimony in court may be restricted. This confirms the Hanafi distinction between tahammul and ada'.
The analogy strengthens the argument by showing a consistent pattern in the school's ruling.

اکرام ہیں اور فاسق اہانت کے لائق ہے لہذا فساق کو گواہ نہ بنا کر ان کی اہانت کی جانی چاہیے ۔ نہ یہ کہ ان کو گواہ بنا کر ان کا اکرام کیا جائے۔ احناف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ فاسق اہل ولایت سے ہے لہذا اہل شہادت
